<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Lightning</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Lightning_(disambiguation)" title="Lightning (disambiguation)">Lightning (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2989" data-file-width="1935" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg/220px-Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg/330px-Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg/440px-Lightning_over_Oradea_Romania_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_Trumbull_County_Ohio.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
High-speed, slow-motion lightning video captured at 6,200 frames per second.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Lightning</b> is a sudden <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_discharge" title="Electrostatic discharge">electrostatic discharge</a> that occurs during a <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a>. This discharge occurs between <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrically charged</a> regions of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thundercloud" title="Thundercloud">cloud</a> (called intra-cloud lightning or IC), between two clouds (CC lightning), or between a cloud and the ground (CG lightning).</p>
<p>The charged regions in the atmosphere temporarily equalize themselves through this discharge referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Lightning_strike" title="Lightning strike"><i>strike</i></a> if it hits an object on the ground, and a <b>flash</b>, if it occurs within a cloud. Lightning creates light in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_body_radiation" title="Black body radiation">black body radiation</a> from the very hot <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> created by the electron flow, and sound in the form of <a href="#Thunder">thunder</a>. Lightning may be seen and not heard when it occurs at a distance too great for the sound to carry as far as the light from the strike or flash.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lightning_electrification">Lightning electrification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Lightning electrification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="261" data-file-width="349" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif/220px-Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif/330px-Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Understanding_Lightning_-_Figure_1_-_Cloud_Charging_Area.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main charging area in a thunderstorm occurs in the central part of the storm where air is moving upward rapidly (updraft) and temperatures range from −15 to −25 Celsius</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The details of the charging process are still being studied by scientists, but there is general agreement on some of the basic concepts of thunderstorm electrification. The main charging area in a thunderstorm occurs in the central part of the storm where air is moving upward rapidly (updraft) and temperatures range from −15 to −25 Celsius, see figure to the right. At that place, the combination of temperature and rapid upward air movement produces a mixture of super-cooled cloud droplets (small water droplets below freezing), small ice crystals, and <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">graupel</a> (soft hail). The updraft carries the super-cooled cloud droplets and very small ice crystals upward. At the same time, the graupel, which is considerably larger and denser, tends to fall or be suspended in the rising air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Graupel_animation_3a.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Graupel_animation_3a.gif/220px-Graupel_animation_3a.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/Graupel_animation_3a.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Graupel_animation_3a.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
When the rising ice crystals collide with graupel (soft hail), the ice crystals become positively charged and the graupel becomes negatively charged.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The differences in the movement of the precipitation cause collisions to occur. When the rising ice crystals collide with graupel (soft hail), the ice crystals become positively charged and the graupel becomes negatively charged. See figure to the left. The updraft carries the positively charged ice crystals upward toward the top of the storm cloud. The larger and denser graupel is either suspended in the middle of the thunderstorm cloud or falls toward the lower part of the storm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charged_cloud_animation_4a.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Charged_cloud_animation_4a.gif/220px-Charged_cloud_animation_4a.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Charged_cloud_animation_4a.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charged_cloud_animation_4a.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The upper part of the thunderstorm cloud becomes positively charged while the middle to lower part of the thunderstorm cloud becomes negatively charged.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The result is that the upper part of the thunderstorm cloud becomes positively charged while the middle to lower part of the thunderstorm cloud becomes negatively charged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The upward motions within the storm and winds at higher levels in the atmosphere tend to cause the small ice crystals (and positive charge) in the upper part of the thunderstorm cloud to spread out horizontally some distance from thunderstorm cloud base. This part of the thunderstorm cloud is called the anvil. While this is the main charging process for the thunderstorm cloud, some of these charges can be redistributed by air movements within the storm (updrafts and downdrafts). In addition, there is a small but important positive charge buildup near the bottom of the thunderstorm cloud due to the precipitation and warmer temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_considerations">General considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: General considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_2" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_3" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv/320px--LightningCNP.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="3.8666545718433" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="LightningCNP.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file, 320 × 240 (244 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="243965" data-framerate="30.000300003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv/LightningCNP.ogv.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="305448" data-framerate="30.000300003"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv/LightningCNP.ogv.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="190624" data-framerate="30.000300003"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:LightningCNP.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv/220px--LightningCNP.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/LightningCNP.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LightningCNP.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Four-second video of a lightning strike, Island in the Sky, <a href="/wiki/Canyonlands_National_Park" title="Canyonlands National Park">Canyonlands National Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a>, United States.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On Earth, the lightning frequency is approximately 40–50 times a second or nearly 1.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1,000,000,000_(number)" title="1,000,000,000 (number)">billion</a> flashes per year<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyWorldClim_freq_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyWorldClim_freq-2">[2]</a></sup> and the average duration is 0.2 seconds made up from a number of much shorter flashes (strokes) of around 30 <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microseconds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many factors affect the frequency, distribution, strength and physical properties of a typical lightning flash in a particular region of the world. These factors include ground elevation, <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prevailing_wind" title="Prevailing wind">prevailing wind</a> currents, <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a>, proximity to warm and cold bodies of water, etc. To a certain degree, the ratio between IC, CC and CG lightning may also vary by <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">season</a> in <a href="/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">middle latitudes</a>. Because human beings are terrestrial and most of their possessions are on the Earth where lightning can damage or destroy them, CG lightning is the most studied and best understood of the three types, even though IC and CC are more common types of lightning. Lightning's relative unpredictability limits a complete explanation of how or why it occurs, even after hundreds of years of scientific investigation.</p>
<p>A typical cloud-to-ground lightning flash culminates in the formation of an electrically conducting <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> channel through the air in excess of 5 kilometres (3.1 mi) tall, from within the cloud to the ground's surface. The actual discharge is the final stage of a very complex process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> At its peak, a typical <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a> produces three or more <i>strikes</i> to the Earth per minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Lightning primarily occurs when warm air is mixed with colder air masses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> resulting in atmospheric disturbances necessary for polarizing the atmosphere.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, it can also occur during <a href="/wiki/Dust_storm" title="Dust storm">dust storms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fires" title="Forest fires">forest fires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic eruptions</a>, and even in the cold of <a href="/wiki/Winter" title="Winter">winter</a>, where the lightning is known as <a href="/wiki/Thundersnow" title="Thundersnow">thundersnow</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">Hurricanes</a> typically generate some lightning, mainly in the rainbands as much as 160 kilometres (99 mi) from the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The science of lightning is called <i>fulminology</i>, and the fear of lightning is called <i><a href="/wiki/Astraphobia" title="Astraphobia">astraphobia</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Frequency">Frequency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Frequency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_lightning" title="Distribution of lightning">Distribution of lightning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_lightning_strikes.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1290" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Global_lightning_strikes.png/300px-Global_lightning_strikes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Global_lightning_strikes.png/450px-Global_lightning_strikes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Global_lightning_strikes.png/600px-Global_lightning_strikes.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_lightning_strikes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World map showing frequency of lightning strikes, in flashes per km² per year (equal-area projection), from combined 1995–2003 data from the Optical Transient Detector and 1998–2003 data from the Lightning Imaging Sensor.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Lightning is not distributed evenly around the planet, as shown in the map.</p>
<p>About 70% of lightning occurs over land in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> where <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">atmospheric convection</a> is the greatest. This occurs from both the mixture of warmer and colder <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">air masses</a>, as well as differences in moisture concentrations, and it generally happens at the <a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">boundaries between them</a>. The flow of warm ocean currents past drier land masses, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_stream" title="Gulf stream">Gulf Stream</a>, partially explains the elevated frequency of lightning in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_United_States" title="Southeast United States">Southeast United States</a>. Because the influence of small or absent land masses in the vast stretches of the world's oceans limits the differences between these variants in the atmosphere, lightning is notably less frequent there than over larger landforms. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_pole" title="North pole">North</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_pole" title="South pole">South Poles</a> are limited in their coverage of thunderstorms and therefore result in areas with the least amount of lightning.</p>
<p>In general, cloud-to-ground (CG) lightning flashes account for only 25% of all total lightning flashes worldwide. Since the base of a thunderstorm is usually negatively charged, this is where most CG lightning originates. This region is typically at the elevation where <a href="/wiki/Freezing_level" title="Freezing level">freezing</a> occurs within the cloud. Freezing, combined with collisions between ice and water, appears to be a critical part of the initial charge development and separation process. During wind-driven collisions, ice crystals tend to develop a positive charge, while a heavier, slushy mixture of ice and water (called <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">graupel</a>) develops a negative charge. Updrafts within a storm cloud separate the lighter ice crystals from the heavier graupel, causing the top region of the cloud to accumulate a positive <a href="/wiki/Space_charge" title="Space charge">space charge</a> while the lower level accumulates a negative space charge.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2566" data-file-width="4288" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg/220px-2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg/330px-2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg/440px-2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2013-08-07_04-23-26-foudre-belfort.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lightning in <a href="/wiki/Belfort" title="Belfort">Belfort</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because the concentrated charge within the cloud must exceed the insulating properties of air, and this increases proportionally to the distance between the cloud and the ground, the proportion of CG strikes (versus cloud-to-cloud (CC) or in-cloud (IC) discharges) becomes greater when the cloud is closer to the ground. In the tropics, where the freezing level is generally higher in the atmosphere, only 10% of lightning flashes are CG. At the latitude of Norway (around 60° North latitude), where the freezing elevation is lower, 50% of lightning is CG.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Lightning is usually produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">cumulonimbus</a> <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">clouds</a>, which have bases that are typically 1–2 km (0.6–1.25 miles) above the ground and tops up to 15 km (9.3 mi) in height.</p>
<p><b>Lightning hotspots</b>: The place on Earth where lightning occurs most often is near the small village of <a href="/wiki/Kifuka" title="Kifuka">Kifuka</a> in the mountains of the eastern <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> where the <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a> is around 975 m (3,200 ft). On average, this region receives 158 lightning strikes per 1 square kilometer (0.39 sq mi) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_freq_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA_freq-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Maracaibo" title="Lake Maracaibo">Lake Maracaibo</a> in Venezuela averages 297 days per year with lightning activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Other lightning hotspots include <a href="/wiki/Catatumbo_lightning" title="Catatumbo lightning">Catatumbo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nea_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nea-18">[18]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lightning_Alley" title="Lightning Alley">Lightning Alley</a> in Central <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alley_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-alley-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fe_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-fe-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Necessary_conditions">Necessary conditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Necessary conditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">Thunderstorm</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="18.416326530612" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Thunder.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_4" preload="none" style="width:220px">
<source data-bandwidth="62333" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (62 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Thunder.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thunder.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sound of a thunderstorm</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In order for an <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_discharge" title="Electrostatic discharge">electrostatic discharge</a> to occur, two preconditions are necessary: firstly, a sufficiently high <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">electric potential</a> between two regions of space must exist, and secondly a high-resistance medium must obstruct the free, unimpeded equalization of the opposite charges.</p>
<p>It is well understood that during a thunderstorm there is charge separation and aggregation in certain regions of the cloud; however the exact processes by which this occurs are not fully understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The atmosphere provides the electrical insulation, or barrier, that prevents free equalization between charged regions of opposite polarity. This is overcome by "lightning", a complex process referred to as the lightning "flash".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_field_generation">Electrical field generation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Electrical field generation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG/220px-LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG/330px-LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/29/LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG/440px-LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LightningAboveCloudsView.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of lightning from an airplane flying above a system.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thundercloud" title="Thundercloud">thundercloud</a> moves over the surface of the Earth, an equal <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a>, but of opposite polarity, is <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_induction" title="Electrostatic induction">induced</a> on the Earth's surface underneath the cloud. The induced positive surface charge, when measured against a fixed point, will be small as the thundercloud approaches, increasing as the center of the storm arrives and dropping as the thundercloud passes. The referential value of the induced surface charge could be roughly represented as a bell curve.</p>
<p>The oppositely charged regions create an <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> within the air between them. This electric field varies in relation to the strength of the surface charge on the base of the thundercloud – the greater the accumulated charge, the higher the electrical field.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flashes_and_strikes">Flashes and strikes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Flashes and strikes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The best studied and understood form of lightning is cloud to ground (CG). Although more common, intracloud (IC) and cloud to cloud (CC) flashes are very difficult to study given there are no "physical" points to monitor inside the clouds. Also, given the very low probability lightning will strike the same point repeatedly and consistently, scientific inquiry is difficult at best even in the areas of high CG frequency. As such, knowing flash propagation is similar amongst all forms of lightning, the best means to describe the process is through an examination of the most studied form, cloud to ground.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desert_Electric.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2441" data-file-width="3887" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Desert_Electric.jpg/250px-Desert_Electric.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Desert_Electric.jpg/375px-Desert_Electric.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Desert_Electric.jpg/500px-Desert_Electric.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Desert_Electric.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A lightning strike from <b>cloud to ground</b> in the California, <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2381" data-file-width="4288" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg/260px-Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg/390px-Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg/520px-Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Florescent_cloud_blanket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An <b>intracloud</b> flash. A lightning flash within the cloud, illuminates the entire blanket.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Downward_leaders">Downward leaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Downward leaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_formation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lightning_formation.gif/220px-Lightning_formation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Lightning_formation.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_formation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A downward leader travels towards earth, branching as it goes.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leaderlightnig.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="420" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leaderlightnig.gif/220px-Leaderlightnig.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Leaderlightnig.gif/330px-Leaderlightnig.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Leaderlightnig.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leaderlightnig.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lightning strike caused by the connection of two leaders, positive shown in blue and negative in red</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In a process not well understood, a channel of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionized" title="Ionized">ionized</a> air, called a "<a href="/wiki/Leader_(spark)" title="Leader (spark)">leader</a>", is initiated from a charged region in the thundercloud. Leaders are electrically conductive channels of partially ionized gas that travel away from a region of dense charge. Negative leaders propagate away from densely charged regions of negative charge, and positive leaders propagate from positively charged regions.</p>
<p>The positively and negatively charged leaders proceed in opposite directions, positive upwards within the cloud and <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">negative</a> towards the earth. Both ionic channels proceed, in their respective directions, in a number of successive spurts. Each leader "pools" ions at the leading tips, shooting out one or more new leaders, momentarily pooling again to concentrate charged ions, then shooting out another leader.</p>
<p>Leaders often split, forming branches in a tree-like pattern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In addition, negative leaders travel in a discontinuous fashion. The resulting jerky movement of these "stepped leader(s)" can be readily observed in slow-motion videos of negative leaders as they head toward ground prior to a negative CG lightning strike. The negative leaders continue to propagate and split as they head downward, often speeding up as they get closer to the Earth's surface.</p>
<p>About 90% of ionic channel lengths between "pools" are approximately 45 m (148 ft) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The establishment of the ionic channel takes a comparatively long amount of time (hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">milliseconds</a>) in comparison to the resulting discharge, which occurs within a few microseconds. The <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">electric current</a> needed to establish the channel, measured in the tens or hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">amperes</a>, is dwarfed by subsequent currents during the actual discharge.</p>
<p>Initiation of the outward leaders is not well understood. The electric field strength within the thundercloud is not typically large enough to initiate this process by itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Many hypotheses have been proposed. One theory postulates that showers of relativistic electrons are created by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays">cosmic rays</a> and are then accelerated to higher velocities via a process called <a href="/wiki/Runaway_breakdown" title="Runaway breakdown">runaway breakdown</a>. As these relativistic electrons collide and ionize neutral air molecules, they initiate leader formation. Another theory invokes locally enhanced electric fields being formed near elongated water droplets or ice crystals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Percolation_theory" title="Percolation theory">Percolation theory</a>, especially for the case of biased percolation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> describes random connectivity phenomena, which produce an evolution of connected structures similar to that of lightning strikes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Upward_streamers">Upward streamers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Upward streamers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>When a stepped leader approaches the ground, the presence of opposite charges on the ground enhances the strength of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a>. The electric field is strongest on grounded objects whose tops are closest to the base of the thundercloud, such as trees and tall buildings. If the electric field is strong enough, a positively charged ionic channel, called a positive or upward <a href="/wiki/Streamer_discharge" title="Streamer discharge">streamer</a>, can develop from these points. This was first theorized by Heinz Kasemir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>As negatively charged leaders approach, increasing the localized electric field strength, grounded objects already experiencing <a href="/wiki/Corona_discharge" title="Corona discharge">corona discharge</a> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_breakdown#Corona_breakdown" title="Electrical breakdown">exceed a threshold</a> and form upward streamers.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attachment">Attachment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Attachment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Once a downward leader connects to an available upward leader, a process referred to as attachment, a low-resistance path is formed and discharge may occur. Photographs have been taken in which unattached streamers are clearly visible. The unattached downward leaders are also visible in branched lightning, none of which are connected to the earth, although it may appear they are.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discharge">Discharge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Discharge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_stroke">Return stroke</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Return stroke">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="239" data-file-width="317" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif/220px-Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lightnings_sequence_2_animation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
High-speed photography showing different parts of a lightning flash during the discharge process as seen in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once a conductive channel bridges the air gap between the negative charge excess in the cloud and the positive surface charge excess below, a massive electrical discharge follows. This is the 'return stroke' and it is the most <a href="/wiki/Luminous_intensity" title="Luminous intensity">luminous</a> and noticeable part of the lightning discharge.</p>
<p>A large electric current flows along the plasma channel from the cloud to the ground, neutralising the positive ground charge as electrons flow away from the strike point to the surrounding area. This huge surge of current creates large radial voltage differences along the surface of the ground. Called step potentials, they are responsible for more injuries and deaths than the strike itself.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Electricity takes every path available to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> A portion of the return stroke current will often preferentially flow through one leg and out another, electrocuting an unlucky human or animal standing near the point where the lightning strikes.</p>
<p>The electric current of the return stroke averages 30 kiloamperes for a typical negative CG flash, often referred to as "negative CG" lightning. In some cases, a ground to cloud (GC) lightning flash may originate from a positively charged region on the ground below a storm. These discharges normally originate from the tops of very tall structures, such as communications antennas. The rate at which the return stroke current travels has been found to be around 1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">8</span></sup> m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>The massive flow of electric current occurring during the return stroke combined with the rate at which it occurs (measured in microseconds) rapidly <a href="/wiki/Superheating" title="Superheating">superheats</a> the completed leader channel, forming a highly electrically conductive plasma channel. The core temperature of the plasma during the return stroke may exceed 50,000 K, causing it to brilliantly radiate with a blue-white color. Once the electric current stops flowing, the channel cools and dissipates over tens or hundreds of milliseconds, often disappearing as fragmented patches of glowing gas. The nearly instantaneous heating during the return stroke causes the air to expand explosively, producing a powerful <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> which is heard as <a href="#Thunder">thunder</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-strike">Re-strike</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Re-strike">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>High-speed videos (examined frame-by-frame) show that most negative CG lightning flashes are made up of 3 or 4 individual strokes, though there may be as many as 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each re-strike is separated by a relatively large amount of time, typically 40 to 50 milliseconds, as other charged regions in the cloud are discharged in subsequent strokes. Re-strikes often cause a noticeable "<a href="/wiki/Strobe_light" title="Strobe light">strobe light</a>" effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uman_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-uman-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each successive stroke is preceded by intermediate dart leader strokes that have a faster rise time but lower amplitude than the initial return stroke. Each subsequent stroke usually re-uses the discharge channel taken by the previous one, but the channel may be offset from its previous position as wind displaces the hot channel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transient_currents_during_flash">Transient currents during flash</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Transient currents during flash">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The electric current within a typical negative CG lightning discharge rises very quickly to its peak value in 1–10 microseconds, then decays more slowly over 50–200 microseconds. The transient nature of the current within a lightning flash results in several phenomena that need to be addressed in the effective protection of ground-based structures. Rapidly changing currents tend to travel on the surface of a conductor, in what is called the <a href="/wiki/Skin_effect" title="Skin effect">skin effect</a>, unlike direct currents, which "flow through" the entire conductor like water through a hose. Hence, conductors used in the protection of facilities tend to be multi-stranded, with small wires woven together. This increases the total bundle <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> in inverse proportion to the individual strand radius, for a fixed total <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">cross-sectional area</a>.</p>
<p>The rapidly changing currents also create <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_pulse" title="Electromagnetic pulse">electromagnetic pulses (EMPs)</a> that radiate outward from the ionic channel. This is a characteristic of all electrical discharges. The radiated pulses rapidly weaken as their distance from the origin increases. However, if they pass over conductive elements such as power lines, communication lines, or metallic pipes, they may induce a current which travels outward to its termination. This is the "<a href="/wiki/Voltage_spike" title="Voltage spike">surge</a>" that, more often than not, results in the destruction of delicate <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_appliance" title="Electrical appliance">electrical appliances</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Electric_motor" title="Electric motor">electric motors</a>. Devices known as <a href="/wiki/Surge_protector" title="Surge protector">surge protectors (SPD) or transient voltage surge suppressors (TVSS)</a> attached in parallel with these lines can detect the lightning flash's transient irregular current, and, through an alteration of its physical properties, route the spike to an attached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_ground" title="Electrical ground">earthing ground</a>, thereby protecting the equipment from damage.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are three primary types of lightning, defined by what is at the "ends" of a flash channel.</p>
<ul>
<li>Intracloud (IC), which occurs within a single thundercloud unit</li>
<li>Cloud to cloud (CC) or intercloud, which starts and ends between two different "functional" thundercloud units</li>
<li>Cloud to ground (CG), that primarily originates in the thundercloud and terminates on an Earth surface, but may also occur in the reverse direction, that is ground to cloud</li>
</ul>
<p>There are variations of each type, such as "positive" versus "negative" CG flashes, that have different physical characteristics common to each which can be measured. Different <a href="/wiki/Common_name" title="Common name">common names</a> used to describe a particular lightning event may be attributed to the same or different events.</p>
<h3><span id="Cloud_to_ground_(CG)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_to_ground_.28CG.29">Cloud to ground (CG)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cloud to ground (CG)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg/220px-Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg/330px-Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e4/Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg/440px-Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lightning_strikes_mountain_top.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Cloud to ground</i> lightning</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cloud-to-ground (CG) lightning is a lightning discharge between a thundercloud and the ground. It is initiated by a stepped leader moving down from the cloud, which met by a streamer moving up from the ground.</p>
<p>CG is the least common, but best understood of all types of lightning. It is easier to study scientifically, because it terminates on a physical object, namely the Earth, and lends itself to being measured by instruments on the ground. Of the three primary types of lightning, it poses the greatest threat to life and property since it terminates or "strikes" the Earth. The overall discharge, termed a strike, is composed of a number of processes such as preliminary breakdown, stepped leaders, connecting leaders, return strokes, dart leaders and subsequent return strokes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive_and_negative_lightning"><span id="Positive_lightning"></span>Positive and negative lightning</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Positive and negative lightning">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Cloud-to-ground (CG) lightning is either positive or negative, as defined by the direction of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_current#Conventions" title="Electric current">conventional electric current</a> from cloud to ground. Most lightning is negative, meaning the ground is more positive. An average bolt of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_polarity" title="Electrical polarity">negative</a> lightning carries an electric current of 30,000 <a href="/wiki/Ampere" title="Ampere">amperes</a> (30 kA), and transfers 15 <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">coulombs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> and 500 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">megajoules</a> of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. Large bolts of lightning can carry up to 120 kA and 350 coulombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="600" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg/220px-Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg/330px-Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg/440px-Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anvil-to-ground_lightning.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anvil-to-ground <i>(Bolt from the blue)</i> lightning strike initiates from the clear, but turbulent sky above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anvil_cloud" title="Anvil cloud">anvil cloud</a>, then drives a bolt of plasma through the cloud directly to the ground.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The majority of cloud-to-ground flashes are "negative" strikes in which a negative charge is transferred to earth. In some cases, a positive charge may be transferred to the ground instead of a negative charge. Unlike the far more common "negative" lightning, positive lightning originates from the positively charged top of the clouds (generally <a href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus_incus" title="Cumulonimbus incus">anvil clouds</a>) rather than the lower portion of the storm. Positive lightning typically makes up less than 5% of all lightning strikes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Positive lightning strikes tend to be much more intense than their negative counterparts; they can produce peak currents up to 300,000 amperes (300 kA) and charges of several hundreds coulombs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of the much greater distance to ground, the positively charged region can develop considerably larger levels of charge and voltages than the negative charge regions in the lower part of the cloud. Positive lightning bolts are considerably hotter and longer than negative lightning. They can develop six to ten times the amount of charge and voltage of a negative bolt and the discharge current may last ten times longer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> A bolt of positive lightning may carry an electric current of 400 kA and the potential at the top of the cloud may exceed a billion volts — about 10 times that of negative lightning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.A.BakshiM.V.Bakshi2009_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.A.BakshiM.V.Bakshi2009-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> During a positive lightning strike, huge quantities of <a href="/wiki/Extremely_low_frequency" title="Extremely low frequency">extremely low frequency</a> (ELF) and <a href="/wiki/Very_low_frequency" title="Very low frequency">very low frequency</a> (VLF) <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a> are generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boccippio_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-boccippio-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of their greater power, as well as lack of warning, positive lightning strikes are considerably more dangerous. At the present time, <a href="/wiki/Aircraft" title="Aircraft">aircraft</a> are not designed to withstand such strikes, since their existence was unknown at the time standards were set, and the dangers unappreciated until the destruction of a <a href="/wiki/Glider_(sailplane)" title="Glider (sailplane)">glider</a> in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The standard in force at the time of the crash, Advisory Circular AC 20-53A, was replaced by Advisory Circular AC 20-53B in 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> however it is unclear whether adequate protection against positive lightning was incorporated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aircraft operating in U.S. airspace have been required to be equipped with <a href="/wiki/Static_discharger" title="Static discharger">static discharge wicks</a>. Although their primary function is to mitigate radio interference due to static buildup through friction with the air, in the event of a lightning strike, a plane is designed to conduct the excess electricity through its skin and structure to the wicks to be safely discharged back into the atmosphere. These measures, however, may be insufficient for positive lightning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Positive lightning has also been shown to trigger the occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Upper-atmospheric_lightning" title="Upper-atmospheric lightning">Upper-atmospheric lightning</a> between the tops of clouds and the ionosphere. Positive lightning tends to occur more frequently in <a href="/wiki/Winter_storm" title="Winter storm">winter storms</a>, as with <a href="/wiki/Thundersnow" title="Thundersnow">thundersnow</a>, during intense <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tornadic_lightning_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tornadic_lightning-47">[47]</a></sup> and in the dissipation stage of a <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHCC_primer2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHCC_primer2-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>A unique form of cloud-to-ground lightning exists where lightning appears to exit from the cumulonimbus cloud and propagate a considerable distance through clear air before veering towards, and striking, the ground. For this reason, they are known as "bolts from the blue". Despite the popular misconception that these are positive lightning strikes due to them seemingly originating from the positive charge region, observations have shown that these are in fact negative flashes. They begin as intracloud flashes within the cloud, the negative leader then exits the cloud from the positive charge region before propagating through clear air and striking the ground some distance away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-onlinelibrary.wiley.com_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-onlinelibrary.wiley.com-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krehbiel.2C_P._R._2008_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krehbiel.2C_P._R._2008-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Cloud_to_cloud_(CC)_and_intra-cloud_(IC)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_to_cloud_.28CC.29_and_intra-cloud_.28IC.29"><span id="Cloud_to_cloud"></span> Cloud to cloud (CC) and intra-cloud (IC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lightning&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cloud to cloud (CC) and intra-cloud (IC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="480" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF/220px-Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF/330px-Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF/440px-Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ligtning_new_delhi_view_1.GIF" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Branching of cloud to cloud lightning, <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg/220px-Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg/330px-Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg/440px-Cloud_to_cloud_lightning_strike.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
